  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_5d588342-a182-49ee-8a5a-0b9f3ee3c467'

<span id="freeTextContainer2111059861073684008">Follow Sir Gibbie on his adventures through the moors of Scotland's Highlands more than a century ago. Having no mother and an alcoholic father, Gibbie must survive on the streets as a child unable to read or speak. See how this boy wins the hearts of his neighbors and offers what little he has to help others. Sir Gibbie teaches adults and children alike about the ability </span>
  <span id="freeText2111059861073684008" style="display:none">Follow Sir Gibbie on his adventures through the moors of Scotland's Highlands more than a century ago. Having no mother and an alcoholic father, Gibbie must survive on the streets as a child unable to read or speak. See how this boy wins the hearts of his neighbors and offers what little he has to help others. Sir Gibbie teaches adults and children alike about the ability to sacrifice self, and to strive for a world more honest and pure than our own.</span>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/8745469-peter-kazmaier">Peter Kazmaier</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">From my recent reading, Ginevra is not Gibbie's cousin, but a more distant Galbraith relative. When Gibbie met Ginevra as part of Miss Kimble's studen<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">From my recent reading, Ginevra is not Gibbie's cousin, but a more distant Galbraith relative. When Gibbie met Ginevra as part of Miss Kimble's student entourage, and was introduced as Sir Gilbert Galbraith, Miss Kimble mistakenly assumed they were cousins because of their similar last names and apparent friendship. Later Miss Kimble insisted that Ginevra had told her Sir Gibbie was her cousin, which Ginevra vehemently denied.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div id="review_154012310" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/154012310" />
    <a title="Chris Shank" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2764185-chris-shank"><img alt="Chris Shank" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598475661p2/2764185.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/154012310?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Shank" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Shank" href="/user/show/2764185-chris-shank">Chris Shank</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer154012310" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5652652348397553805">MacDonald is my favorite author, and he rarely disappoints. I can be a very critical personality, but MacDonald is the one author I have grown to trust almost implicitly. I always learn from him, and he always refreshes my vision and power to see beauty in the world. He makes me feel like he can actually makes sense of the cosmos, and does a better job than I’ve EVER witnessed of integrating the sorrow and pain of life into a worldview in which life would be less without the presence of sufferin</span>
  <span id="freeText5652652348397553805" style="display:none">MacDonald is my favorite author, and he rarely disappoints. I can be a very critical personality, but MacDonald is the one author I have grown to trust almost implicitly. I always learn from him, and he always refreshes my vision and power to see beauty in the world. He makes me feel like he can actually makes sense of the cosmos, and does a better job than I’ve EVER witnessed of integrating the sorrow and pain of life into a worldview in which life would be less without the presence of suffering and questions. His writings bring peace, while challenging me to accept new risks, and I always try to have books of his in my reading pile at least a few times a year. <br /><br />Sir Gibbie was another exceptional read with poetic brilliance and ideological diamonds buried throughout. I generally feel that GM’s plot builds around and subordinately to his central proposition and philosophy (his ‘sermon’), but this storyline was almost as profound as the morals and one-liners. <br /><br />Sir Gibbie is about a mute child that is orphaned and homeless, living off the charity of others initially, ultimately finding his fortunes change as he comes of age, and rising to a station of influence. His is a life you can see straight to the bottom of, teeming with the clear and life-giving waters of love and hope. But he is no pushover—his robust physical/spiritual stature comes to the aid of the weak and poor around him. Gibbie is pure in heart…almost too pure for a reader’s cynicism. MacDonald acknowledges this by offering a defense for the obvious, improbable nature of such a wholesome persona that exemplifies good and true humanity: “It is the noble, not the failure from the noble, that is the true human. I have attempted to portray] a man trying to be merely as noble as is absolutely essential to his being.” I loved following this protagonist as other characters ‘bumped’ into him, astonished to find in him a deeply beautiful nature without the trappings and dysfunctions of vapid socialites.<br /><br />A few quotes from the book may be the best way to evidence what I believe to be the unrivaled genius of MacDonald’s works, <br /><br />“Hardy through hardship, he knew nothing better than a constant good-humored sparring with nature and circumstance for the privilege of being.” <br />“Most children would rather be struck and kissed alternately than neither.”<br />“When life begins to speculate upon itself, I suspect it has begun to die.”<br />“Every honest cry, even if sent through the deaf ears of an idol, passes on to the ears of the unknown God, the heart of the unknown Father.”<br />“She had found that her brains were never worth much to her until her heart took up the education of them.”<br />“The one secret of life and development is not to devise and plan, but to fall in with the forces at work—to do every moment’s duty aright.”<br />“To try too hard to make people good is one way to make them worse; the only way to make them good is to be good…the time for speaking comes rarely, the time for being never departs.”<br />“For when a man spends his energy on appearing to have, he is all the time destroying what he has.”<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5652652348397553805" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/199651584" />
    <a title="Nathan C." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2992626-nathan-c"><img alt="Nathan C." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1322001270p2/2992626.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/281240123?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer281240123" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1178649511333382231">This book is one of my absolute favorites. Although, I'm ashamed to admit that I found a free version of it online - I'm looking for an antique copy to put on my bookshelf.<br/>The story, simply put, is beautiful. We follow the struggles and lessons of a joyously innocent mute boy through childhood into adulthood and meet the most colorful characters in between - some loathsome and some heartwarmingly precious. I read this over Christmas break during my freshman year in college, and the timing couldn</span>
  <span id="freeText1178649511333382231" style="display:none">This book is one of my absolute favorites. Although, I'm ashamed to admit that I found a free version of it online - I'm looking for an antique copy to put on my bookshelf.<br />The story, simply put, is beautiful. We follow the struggles and lessons of a joyously innocent mute boy through childhood into adulthood and meet the most colorful characters in between - some loathsome and some heartwarmingly precious. I read this over Christmas break during my freshman year in college, and the timing couldn't have been more appropriate; although the story doesn't take place during Christmas, it has the blissful magic of the Christmas season throughout.<br />Language geeks will get a kick out of figuring out the Scottish brogue, especially those interested in the history of the English language. I was pleasantly surprised to see the similarity between German and the Scottish brogue, like a middle child between modern English and German.<br />Altogether, this book is so worth the read; I'd encourage everyone to read it.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5843203497754776767">I read this book over and over. George MacDonald is one of my favorite writers. The one thing that prevented his writing from being widely distributed is that he wrote in Scottish brogue, so it's incredibly difficult to get through. This edition (given a different title than his origninal title, Sir Gibbie, a 1927 edition on my bookshelf, has been marketed to the young adult audience, but my husband and I both agree it is the best story we have ever read.   <span id="freeText5843203497754776767" style="display:none">I read this book over and over. George MacDonald is one of my favorite writers. The one thing that prevented his writing from being widely distributed is that he wrote in Scottish brogue, so it's incredibly difficult to get through. This edition (given a different title than his origninal title, Sir Gibbie, a 1927 edition on my bookshelf, has been marketed to the young adult audience, but my husband and I both agree it is the best story we have ever read. We read it aloud together four years ago. It is a spiritually-based story, which isn't surprising, given MacDonald was C.S. Lewis' favorite author. Mine, too.</span>

<div id="review_1561019145" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1561019145" />
    <a title="Manuel Alfonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35970992-manuel-alfonseca"><img alt="Manuel Alfonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420279369p2/35970992.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1561019145?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manuel Alfonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manuel Alfonseca" href="/user/show/35970992-manuel-alfonseca">Manuel Alfonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1561019145" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3251136197780065695">George MacDonald did it again: he shows that the ways of God are not our ways, that our reasons are not necessarily God's reasons.<br/><br/>The book shows a complex cast of characters from all the social levels: from the laird to the homeless, going through a couple of priests, one of them prone to yield to worldly considerations, although he's good enough, or clever enough, not to fall into wickedness; the other merely pompous and self-righteous.<br/><br/>But the aim of the story (if such a concept has a meaning) </span>
  <span id="freeText3251136197780065695" style="display:none">George MacDonald did it again: he shows that the ways of God are not our ways, that our reasons are not necessarily God's reasons.<br /><br />The book shows a complex cast of characters from all the social levels: from the laird to the homeless, going through a couple of priests, one of them prone to yield to worldly considerations, although he's good enough, or clever enough, not to fall into wickedness; the other merely pompous and self-righteous.<br /><br />But the aim of the story (if such a concept has a meaning) is to show that our cherished idea, that bad people are the almost inevitable result of an unjust social treatment, is simply wrong. <br /><br />Wee Sir Gibbie has all the numbers to become one of the dregs of society: a drunkard father; as a child he gets in a den of assassins; in rags and poverty, he flies away; then he is punished for having done good to others.<br /><br />But he never falters, and as soon as he is taught about the Gospels, he receives them eagerly and becomes a Christ-figure, a knight-errant, a wrong-righter. The only question in my mind is whether it is possible for a mere human to be so Christ-like.<br /><br />I don't like this book as much as "The princess &amp; the goblin." Paradoxically enough there's something unreal in this realistic novel (Sir Gibbie) that is not in the fantasy book, but I'd put it almost at the level of "At the back of the North Wind," although the latter is a book for children and "Sir Gibbie" is a book for adults.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3251136197780065695" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_616501162" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/616501162" />
    <a title="Elizabeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6381340-elizabeth"><img alt="Elizabeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522622176p2/6381340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/616501162?book_show_action=true">May 15, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth" href="/user/show/6381340-elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19103730-sir-gibbie">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6381340-elizabeth?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer616501162" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16911427494886432900">I don't consider sitting down and writing a review of a book that was finished less than half an hour ago the wisest of moves. Almost any work deserves more reflection than that teensy period of time allows, and "Sir Gibbie" certainly does. Here I am, though. <br/><br/>The works of George MacDonald (whether fiction or non) tend to inject life and light directly into my soul whenever I read them. They provide, in my eyes, the most glorious expositions of truth and beauty. In "Sir Gibbie" I found almost mor</span>
  <span id="freeText16911427494886432900" style="display:none">I don't consider sitting down and writing a review of a book that was finished less than half an hour ago the wisest of moves. Almost any work deserves more reflection than that teensy period of time allows, and "Sir Gibbie" certainly does. Here I am, though. <br /><br />The works of George MacDonald (whether fiction or non) tend to inject life and light directly into my soul whenever I read them. They provide, in my eyes, the most glorious expositions of truth and beauty. In "Sir Gibbie" I found almost more of this than I could bear. It is a rich, rich novel and has stirred me more than any I have read for quite a long time. It isn't exactly easy to read, but I didn't have to persevere to finish it, as I thought I might in the beginning. The dialogue is challenging (I admit to skimming over parts of it) but worth the effort. It catches you up, eventually, and takes you with it. <br /><br />Though I usually make a point not to recommend books to people, this much is safe to say: If you enjoy Mr. MacDonald's other works (or those of the many writers he inspired), it would be a grievous shame not to take up a copy of "Sir Gibbie". </span>
  <a data-text-id="16911427494886432900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1230978555966419822">It’s for good reason that both C.S. Lewis and JRR Tolkien count George MacDonald as a mentor. Mr. MacDonald is a master storyteller and in this book has recounted the life of wee Sir Gibbie, an orphan who cannot speak and with no advantages (as the world counts them). Gibbie flees the city and is taken in by a simple country family where he finds love, friendship, and God. <br/><br/>Gibbie’s simple faith in God’s Word ignites his love for people and his sacrifice to help all those in need. It’s an inspiri</span>
  <span id="freeText1230978555966419822" style="display:none">It’s for good reason that both C.S. Lewis and JRR Tolkien count George MacDonald as a mentor. Mr. MacDonald is a master storyteller and in this book has recounted the life of wee Sir Gibbie, an orphan who cannot speak and with no advantages (as the world counts them). Gibbie flees the city and is taken in by a simple country family where he finds love, friendship, and God. <br /><br />Gibbie’s simple faith in God’s Word ignites his love for people and his sacrifice to help all those in need. It’s an inspiring book, a joy to read, and a wonderful story. Highly recommended, especially for the young adult.<br /><br />Note: George MacDonald is an amazing author; however, many of his books are written in Scottish. Michael Philips does edits them so that they’re readable and accessible to today’s reader. </span>

<div id="review_3454266366" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3454266366" />
    <a title="J.F." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10850424-j-f"><img alt="J.F." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534152780p2/10850424.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3454266366?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.F." class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.F." href="/user/show/10850424-j-f">J.F.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/43130059-sir-gibbie">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3454266366" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13601487306032188252">When I learned of George MacDonald's influence on the father's of Christian fantasy--J R R Tolkien and C S Lewis, I had to read some of his work. Sir Gibbie came highly recommended.<br/><br/>Sir Gibbie comes to life in such a way that is rare for most characters in a book. I got to watch him grow up and experience what this pure soul experienced. And unlike most characters, though Gibbie certainly grows, he doesn't change much. But watching how his pure actions affect those around him is incredible and in</span>
  <span id="freeText13601487306032188252" style="display:none">When I learned of George MacDonald's influence on the father's of Christian fantasy--J R R Tolkien and C S Lewis, I had to read some of his work. Sir Gibbie came highly recommended.<br /><br />Sir Gibbie comes to life in such a way that is rare for most characters in a book. I got to watch him grow up and experience what this pure soul experienced. And unlike most characters, though Gibbie certainly grows, he doesn't change much. But watching how his pure actions affect those around him is incredible and inspiring.<br /><br />And how often did I wish he would speak to explain himself and free himself from awful situations! But he wouldn't...and I'm glad he didn't. His actions were more powerful without words--so much so that I have to wonder how often I speak when I should simply remain silent.<br /><br />Sir Gibbie is more than a story. He is a person...alive in my imagination, inspiring me still to be loving, generous, and...sometimes...silent.<br /><br />If you have never read Sir Gibbie and you want to dive deep into his world, make a new friend and be inspired--read Sir Gibbie. And I recommend this version due to the Scottish translations which aren't always self explanatory.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13601487306032188252" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_165469489" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/165469489" />
    <a title="Michelle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4581174-michelle"><img alt="Michelle" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/165469489?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michelle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michelle" href="/user/show/4581174-michelle">Michelle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer165469489" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14030554544065965093">Brilliantly written novel. George MacDonald is such a deep thinker and this novel is a discussion basically of the human heart and it's reaction to Jesus' love. A typical Victorian protagonist, Gibbie, is almost Christ-like in his pure heart, but he provides the visualisation of a pure heart's reaction to Jesus as a comparison with other characters.<br/>The story is very sweet, but satisfying.<br/>The broad Scots language makes those parts of the dialogue quite heavy reading, until I got into the rhythmn </span>
  <span id="freeText14030554544065965093" style="display:none">Brilliantly written novel. George MacDonald is such a deep thinker and this novel is a discussion basically of the human heart and it's reaction to Jesus' love. A typical Victorian protagonist, Gibbie, is almost Christ-like in his pure heart, but he provides the visualisation of a pure heart's reaction to Jesus as a comparison with other characters.<br />The story is very sweet, but satisfying.<br />The broad Scots language makes those parts of the dialogue quite heavy reading, until I got into the rhythmn and style of the language and then I read it to myself in the correct accent and I really enjoyed the feel and musicality of the speech.<br />There were some very deep thoughts on subjects like love in marriage, forgiveness and thinking of others as better than yourselves which were slipped into the narrative and really gave you something to think about. Each complex thought was written concisely and extremely articulately. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14030554544065965093" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2792539080383272600" style="display:none">SIR GIBBIE "tells the enchanting story of....a seemingly destitute orphan, whose life - thought he is unable to speak - communicates truth, innocent love, and goodness." <br /><br />I especially love this version of the book because it is not edited at all, so the full color and the rich colloquialisms of the Scottish Highlands come through. You may have trouble at first trying to decipher what words such as cantrip, gowan, tarn, and hirsute refer to, but before long, you'll find yourself understanding every word.<br /><br />GEORGE MACDONALD (1824-1905) "Scottish born poet, preacher and novelist, was one of the original and most influential writers of Victorian Britain. He wrote over fifty books with millions of copies sold and was one of the most popular authors on both sides of the Atlantic. C.S. Lewis once said of George MacDonald, 'I regarded him as my master.'"<br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18079659203156349782">Sir Gibbie or otherwise known as The Baronet’s Song is a book that I really enjoyed reading. I liked it much better than it’s sequel The Shepherd’s Castle (don’t ask me why I read the second book first… sometimes I just do weird things… xD) <br/>From the start, I fell in love with the little half-naked urchin that roamed the streets, needing much, but wanting nothing.   <span id="freeText18079659203156349782" style="display:none">Sir Gibbie or otherwise known as The Baronet’s Song is a book that I really enjoyed reading. I liked it much better than it’s sequel The Shepherd’s Castle (don’t ask me why I read the second book first… sometimes I just do weird things… xD) <br />From the start, I fell in love with the little half-naked urchin that roamed the streets, needing much, but wanting nothing. The hardships he endured as a child and young adult will melt your heart and yet he was satisfied to love, though he was not loved back and to help others in need, though almost no one helped him in return. Throughout the book Gibbie is simple, yet intelligent; he becomes rich, yet remains humble; he gives all he can! He learns of Christ and becomes His follower, walking in His footsteps, though others try to stop him. <br /></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer93791989" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer18390699736976164692">1st Read 2*<br/>2nd Read2*<br/><br/>My father read this book to us when we were kids, so when I saw it on Librivox I thought I would give it another go. I'm a fan of George MacDonald - his children's fantasy stories are great. "Sir Gibbie" isn't the same, it's more a rags-to-riches story in the tone of the time - where a paragon of virtue finally gets the good that he deserves. I don't mind this sort of thing, if done in moderation, it's a way of getting across a point - and MacDonald's theme of unselfishness </span>
  <span id="freeText18390699736976164692" style="display:none">1st Read 2*<br />2nd Read2*<br /><br />My father read this book to us when we were kids, so when I saw it on Librivox I thought I would give it another go. I'm a fan of George MacDonald - his children's fantasy stories are great. "Sir Gibbie" isn't the same, it's more a rags-to-riches story in the tone of the time - where a paragon of virtue finally gets the good that he deserves. I don't mind this sort of thing, if done in moderation, it's a way of getting across a point - and MacDonald's theme of unselfishness is something I most definitely hold dear - but a character without flaw is not realistic, not believable, not convincing. Perhaps this would have read a lot different in MacDonald's day. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18390699736976164692" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15820657498355863384">Possibly one of my favorite books of all time. Thoughtful, old-fashioned, and at time difficult to read due to the heavy Scottish brogue in the dialogue. Is Gibbie as a character, too good to be true? Of course. But MacDonald uses him to advantage to teach his readers the Christian way of life.</span>

          </span>

        <a title="Frank-Intergalactic Bookdragon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Frank-Intergalactic Bookdragon" href="/user/show/68404236-frank-intergalactic-bookdragon">Frank-Intergalactic Bookdragon</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68404236-frank-intergalactic-bookdragon?shelf=classics">classics</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2492800923" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15994670763562038930">So I'm gonna keep this short because I don't have that many thoughts on this, here's the things I do want to say:<br/><br/>-it is very well written, I feel like often classics seem very pretentious and wordy but I feel like this was the right balance of intelligent writing and easy to read and understand.<br/><br/>-it's short!! A short classic!!! If only the font weren't so tiny . . .<br/><br/>-I do wish the font was bigger because that +having less than a week to read this (well TECHNICALLY I had two months but I procrastin</span>
  <span id="freeText15994670763562038930" style="display:none">So I'm gonna keep this short because I don't have that many thoughts on this, here's the things I do want to say:<br /><br />-it is very well written, I feel like often classics seem very pretentious and wordy but I feel like this was the right balance of intelligent writing and easy to read and understand.<br /><br />-it's short!! A short classic!!! If only the font weren't so tiny . . .<br /><br />-I do wish the font was bigger because that +having less than a week to read this (well TECHNICALLY I had two months but I procrastinated, why am I like this????) I found myself skimming unintentionally way to much, I think that's why I don't have much to say because I don't feel like I entirely understood the story.<br /><br />-I get what MacDonald was trying to do with Gibbie and trying to make him a Christ-like figure (this is technically Christian literature which despite me being Christian myself I don't often read because reasons I'm about to explain) but honestly is ANYONE inspired by characters who have no internal problems? Gibbie is good inside and out, which I have nothing against good characters, but he never has ANY temptation??? Really?? There is literally not one flaw about him. This is why I don't often read Christian fiction because it can be preachy in a way that doesn't help. Even Jesus was tempted, several times, by Satan himself. So this is telling me that Gibbie, a little kid in an extremely rough situation, never even considers sinning???? Honestly isn't it more inspiring if a character has flaws like a normal human being yet ends up victorious anyway? Did I miss his flaws somewhere?*<br /><br />*<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[and no I will not consider a possible genetic disposition for alcoholism as a flaw because a) he is never tempted to drink, he knows well enough not to by seeing how destructive it was on his father and b) this was written before they had a good understanding of genetics, I'm not a historian so I don't know if they considered alcoholism a trait you can pass down but I doubt it. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10364041-kelsey-bryant?shelf=christian-fiction">christian-fiction</a>,

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3113337938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14093816336613234553">This is one of those books I liked so much and felt so awed by that it's hard to write a review for it... so I'll keep it simple for now. I grew up thinking that MacDonald's Scottish novels in their original form were inaccessible ... but that is certainly not the case, especially now with David Jack's side-by-side translation of the Doric dialect into English. MacDonald's works are profound and beautiful, his language flowing and poetic, and I'm so glad they are being republished like this. I'm</span>
  <span id="freeText14093816336613234553" style="display:none">This is one of those books I liked so much and felt so awed by that it's hard to write a review for it... so I'll keep it simple for now. I grew up thinking that MacDonald's Scottish novels in their original form were inaccessible ... but that is certainly not the case, especially now with David Jack's side-by-side translation of the Doric dialect into English. MacDonald's works are profound and beautiful, his language flowing and poetic, and I'm so glad they are being republished like this. I'm eager to read more of them, though it's hard to imagine that I'll like any of them better than <i>Sir Gibbie</i>. <br /><br />All this novel's characters are memorable and meaningful, but none more so than Sir Gibbie himself, a mute boy with a heart full of love for mankind. He has such a beautiful and inspiring story! </span>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2599549201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10651991569132105363">What a delightful read in this splendid translation by David Jack! MacDonald challenges us to know what goodness really is through the eyes of wee Gibbie. Could it be innate? We meet those who immediately love him and those who don't, but in their midst we do understand that love, real love, is the only thing that makes us good. <br/><br/>I enjoyed reading of Glashgar, its surrounds, and its people. So many relationships, sermonettes, and adventures (the flood!) fill these pages. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1218808963" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1218808963" />
    <a title="Leah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5566516-leah"><img alt="Leah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395281213p2/5566516.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1218808963?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leah" href="/user/show/5566516-leah">Leah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19103730-sir-gibbie">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5566516-leah?shelf=george-macdonald">george-macdonald</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1218808963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6308683069824880998">For about the first half of this book I kept switching back and forth between the original version (Sir Gibbie) and the version edited by Michael Phillips (The Baronet's Song). I then switched to the edited version to finish it while I was on vacation. <br/><br/>I do think that Michael Phillips did a good job of editing and his versions would be great for those who like to quickly devour inspirational fiction. He basically translated the dialect and cut out a lot of the moral ramblings and long descriptio</span>
  <span id="freeText6308683069824880998" style="display:none">For about the first half of this book I kept switching back and forth between the original version (Sir Gibbie) and the version edited by Michael Phillips (The Baronet's Song). I then switched to the edited version to finish it while I was on vacation. <br /><br />I do think that Michael Phillips did a good job of editing and his versions would be great for those who like to quickly devour inspirational fiction. He basically translated the dialect and cut out a lot of the moral ramblings and long descriptions so that mostly what was left was the action of the story.<br /><br />However, for me, the sweet spot would be changing the archaic Scottish dialect to an easier Scottish accent, leaving in all the beautiful descriptions, and cutting out about 25% of the moral ramblings. <br /><br />When I looked back through my highlighted quotes on the original version, most of them were not found in the edited version. So it IS lacking some of what I most enjoy. <br /><br />The original felt very similar to reading Elizabeth Goudge. He rambles quite a bit more than her, but their descriptive style and way of finding spiritual encouragement in the beauty of everyday life are similar.<br /><br />Here's an example of the dialect: "The corn's 'maist cuttit noo," replied Robert. "But he needna gang ilka day. He can gie ae day to the learnin', an' the neist to thinkin' aboot it amo' the sheep. An' ony day 'at ye want to keep him, ye can keep him; for it winna be as gien he gaed to the schuil." I CAN get it, it's just that my brain has enough to chew on with his wonderful spiritual lessons that I don't want a challenge when it comes to the dialogue. I suppose I'd get used to it with practice.<br /><br />Here are a couple short quotes that I liked: "The child way of saying must always lie nearer the child way of seeing, which is the poetic way."<br />and "It is the heart that is not yet sure of its God, that is afraid to laugh in His presence." There were several others I liked but they were much longer and I'm trying to keep this review short.<br /><br />I did love his writing and I can see why he was one of C.S. Lewis' favorite authors. I will be savoring more of his novels as I find time in the coming years.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6308683069824880998" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Kathy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2376673-kathy"><img alt="Kathy" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58476865?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathy" rel="nofollow" href="/user/show/2376673-kathy">Kathy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780871232915" href="/book/show/1018666.The_Baronet_s_Song">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58476865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10768140376328025465">In The Baronet's Song, MacDonald grants us a vision of a neglected Scottish waif. Born a mute city urchin to a drunken cobbler with an empty title, Gibbie ekes out his existence through the charity of kind hearted people about town. When his father dies, circumstances drive him to the country and an uncertain future.<br/>Wee Sir Gibbie's innocence and purity as a child and as an adult are striking. Those traits stuck with me as an underlying current through the entire read and after I put the book do</span>
  <span id="freeText10768140376328025465" style="display:none">In The Baronet's Song, MacDonald grants us a vision of a neglected Scottish waif. Born a mute city urchin to a drunken cobbler with an empty title, Gibbie ekes out his existence through the charity of kind hearted people about town. When his father dies, circumstances drive him to the country and an uncertain future.<br />Wee Sir Gibbie's innocence and purity as a child and as an adult are striking. Those traits stuck with me as an underlying current through the entire read and after I put the book down. From his intentional purity springs his ability to love. MacDonald writes at a key point that because Gibbie spent his entire life loving every person he met with everything he had, his ability to love was expanded. The idea is practical and revolutionary at the same time. Sometimes we think that if we invest ourselves in fewer people, our relationships will be stronger. That is oftentimes true. However, if we are able to give of our time, our energies, and our friendship-love to even strangers we meet on the street, would that not deepen our capacity for love even more?</span>
  <a data-text-id="10768140376328025465" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer604901836" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer593338257910568866"> C.S. Lewis considered George MacDonald his "master," and his works of fantasy are some of my favorite books. Lewis also recognized that MacDonald's realistic fiction wasn't as strong: "Necessity made MacDonald a novelist, but few of his novels are good and none is very good. They are best when they depart most from the canons of novel writing... Sometimes they depart in order to come nearer to fantasy, as in the whole character of the hero in Sir Gibbie..."<br/><br/>This novel is full of broad Scots dial</span>
  <span id="freeText593338257910568866" style="display:none"> C.S. Lewis considered George MacDonald his "master," and his works of fantasy are some of my favorite books. Lewis also recognized that MacDonald's realistic fiction wasn't as strong: "Necessity made MacDonald a novelist, but few of his novels are good and none is very good. They are best when they depart most from the canons of novel writing... Sometimes they depart in order to come nearer to fantasy, as in the whole character of the hero in Sir Gibbie..."<br /><br />This novel is full of broad Scots dialect, and the plot is often slow, so I must admit that I struggled to keep reading at times. The hero is a penniless mute orphan, who, when he is beaten for doing good, cannot cry out. He loves humanity and seeks the good of others, but he is often misunderstood. It was like a slow, then sudden sunrise when I saw the character of Christ revealed. I'm grateful for nonfiction and theology, but I'm especially glad of books that wake up the imagination and show the loveliness of our savior.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4212157891594536946">It's hard to know exactly what to say, other than that I loved it! It was different than any other book I've read, yet similar to the best Christian fiction I know of. The young Baronet, Gibbie, is a delight. His heart is pure and his life goal is to serve God with all his resources, both when he is young and penniless and when he comes into his inheritance. He loves people with God's love. It's a beautiful story; it made me laugh, ponder, and get misty-eyed. The characters, especially Gibbie, f</span>
  <span id="freeText4212157891594536946" style="display:none">It's hard to know exactly what to say, other than that I loved it! It was different than any other book I've read, yet similar to the best Christian fiction I know of. The young Baronet, Gibbie, is a delight. His heart is pure and his life goal is to serve God with all his resources, both when he is young and penniless and when he comes into his inheritance. He loves people with God's love. It's a beautiful story; it made me laugh, ponder, and get misty-eyed. The characters, especially Gibbie, fill your imagination and behind them rises the lovely land of Scotland. George MacDonald's love for God and His Gospel, for people, for storytelling, and for Scotland shine forth. He wrote many books, and they'll be a source I can turn to again and again for a warm, uplifting, thoughtful, and imaginative read.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4374495607076738329">A beautiful story! Gibbie, the rightful heir of the property, has a loving heart that likes to serve people despite his own dire circumstances.   <span id="freeText4374495607076738329" style="display:none">A beautiful story! Gibbie, the rightful heir of the property, has a loving heart that likes to serve people despite his own dire circumstances. He serves the drunkards, Jean, Janet, Robert, Donal and many others. I believe that the author is conveying the message that because of his humble heart and spirit, God blesses him by restoring to him his ancient family property. Ginevra, who had compassion on Gibbie and helped him when he was mistreated by her father, is blessed also and ends up being his wife. If she had been as her father was, and scorned Gibbie, this would never have happened. Have compassion and be humble even to those that appear to have nothing. God is watching. This is what I got out of the story. First I read the edited version and because it was so good, bought and read the original version published by CreateSpace Independent Publishing.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6525319822763703080">Although it wasn't a perfect book (a bit wordy in places, Scottish dialect was so thick that it was distracting, and some of the descriptions went over my head), it was so good that I can't help but give it five stars. What was good about it? I loved Gibbie. He made me want to be loving and good in the very best sense. As much as it showed goodness, it also showed badness for what it is, selfishness, weakness, and pride. There were so many truths and insights sprinkled throughout. The romance wa</span>
  <span id="freeText6525319822763703080" style="display:none">Although it wasn't a perfect book (a bit wordy in places, Scottish dialect was so thick that it was distracting, and some of the descriptions went over my head), it was so good that I can't help but give it five stars. What was good about it? I loved Gibbie. He made me want to be loving and good in the very best sense. As much as it showed goodness, it also showed badness for what it is, selfishness, weakness, and pride. There were so many truths and insights sprinkled throughout. The romance was very good, although I don't typically prefer it. It was palatable because it wasn't the central theme but showed it, like goodness and badness, for what it is. The story itself, at times predictable, was still delightful and had some surprising twists peppered throughout. George MacDonald is definitely among my top three favorite writers. </span>

<div id="review_388437326" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/388437326" />
    <a title="Karoline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3630502-karoline"><img alt="Karoline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1326423660p2/3630502.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/388437326?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karoline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karoline" href="/user/show/3630502-karoline">Karoline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9143978-sir-gibbie">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer388437326" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4766328867754416476">So hard to rate this one. So many good things and so many inferior things rub elbows that I can hardly generalize. I'll just say this, that I've never been able to be sorry I spent time reading MacDonald. There's always something worth taking away, something wholesome and refreshing and lovely, however much saccharinity, error, or irrelevance attends it. John Buchan said of Walter Scott that he had the power of sophrosyne - saving thoughts, and spoke of a "lovely goodness" in his writing. That's</span>
  <span id="freeText4766328867754416476" style="display:none">So hard to rate this one. So many good things and so many inferior things rub elbows that I can hardly generalize. I'll just say this, that I've never been able to be sorry I spent time reading MacDonald. There's always something worth taking away, something wholesome and refreshing and lovely, however much saccharinity, error, or irrelevance attends it. John Buchan said of Walter Scott that he had the power of sophrosyne - saving thoughts, and spoke of a "lovely goodness" in his writing. That's a good phrase for MacDonald - a thread of "lovely goodness" runs through his imagination, and shows in all his writings. If you come to his books to appreciate that, and can discard the irrelevancies and ignore the plot (heh) - then I don't think you'll feel, either, that you've wasted time. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4766328867754416476" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Sk0bOZ4Q87hee8P2SiD7Mds6TtgEpiaR3WR/F4lphtDfJG8L21+ySYcXIHxCGwtv8pnfgcgzSdDAk4082eeSeA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Written by the American author and educator Kate Douglas Wiggin, it is the story of young and poor Rebecca Rowena Randall, who goes to live with her spinster aunts in the town of Riverboro when she is ten years-old. Rebecca’s father had died three years before and the family farm had become heavily indebted. In order to ease the burden on her widowed mother, Rebecca is sent to live with her lonely aunts at their farm and there she spends the next seven years till she becomes an adult. Rebecca brings her youthful enthusiasm and imagination to their quiet life and often clashes with her stern Aunt Miranda. Yet, Rebecca finds love and acceptance with her Aunt Jane and she grows up to be a proper and intelligent young lady who never loses her sunny outlook and kind heart. “Rebecca of Sunnybrook Farm” is a classic coming of age story that has been adapted numerous times for the stage and screen and continues to charm audiences and readers alike with its interesting characters and positive message of finding happiness in life’s simple pleasures.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"9221448951081332541\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div If so, you’re rather psychologically peculiar.Unlike much of the world today, and most people who have ever lived, WEIRD people are highly individualistic, self-obsessed, control-oriented, nonconformist, and analytical. They focus on themselves—their attributes, accomplishments, and aspirations—over their relationships and social roles. How did WEIRD populations become so psychologically distinct? What role did these psychological differences play in the industrial revolution and the global expansion of Europe during the last few centuries?In The WEIRDest People in the World, Joseph Henrich draws on cutting-edge research in anthropology, psychology, economics, and evolutionary biology to explore these questions and more. He illuminates the origins and evolution of family structures, marriage, and religion, and the profound impact these cultural transformations had on human psychology. Mapping these shifts through ancient history and late antiquity, Henrich reveals that the most fundamental institutions of kinship and marriage changed dramatically under pressure from the Roman Catholic Church. It was these changes that gave rise to the WEIRD psychology that would coevolve with impersonal markets, occupational specialization, and free competition—laying the foundation for the modern world.Provocative and engaging in both its broad scope and its surprising details, The WEIRDest People in the World explores how culture, institutions, and psychology shape one another, and explains what this means for both our most personal sense of who we are as individuals and also the large-scale social, political, and economic forces that drive human history. \n  Include black-and-white The lions and pythons that prowled beyond the village fences were the greatest threat they knew. All that changed the night the government-armed Murahiliin began attacking their villages.\n\nAmid the chaos, screams, conflagration, and gunfire, 5-year-old Benson and 7-year-old Benjamin fled into the dark night. Two years later, Alepho, age 7, was forced to do the same. Across the Southern Sudan, over the next 5 years, thousands of other boys did likewise, joining this stream of child refugees that became known as the Lost Boys. Their journey would take them more than 1000 miles across a war-ravaged country, through landmine-sown paths, crocodile-infested waters, and grotesque extremes of hunger, thirst, and disease. The refugee camps they eventually filtered through offered little respite from the brutality they were fleeing.\n\nIn They Poured Fire on Us From the Sky, Alepho, Benson, and Benjamin, by turn, recount their experiences along this unthinkable journey. They vividly recall the family, friends, and tribal world they left far behind them and their desperate efforts to keep track of one another. This is a captivating memoir of Sudan and a powerful portrait of war as seen through the eyes of children.           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4134414265339320724">George MacDonald was a Scottish author, poet, and Christian minister.<br/><br/>He was educated at Aberdeen University and after a short and stormy career as a minister at Arundel, where his unorthodox views led to his dismissal, he turned to fiction as a means of earning a living. He wrote over 50 books.<br/><br/>Known particularly for his poignant fairy tales and fantasy novels, MacDonald inspired many authors, such</span>
  <span id="freeText4134414265339320724" style="display:none">George MacDonald was a Scottish author, poet, and Christian minister.<br /><br />He was educated at Aberdeen University and after a short and stormy career as a minister at Arundel, where his unorthodox views led to his dismissal, he turned to fiction as a means of earning a living. He wrote over 50 books.<br /><br />Known particularly for his poignant fairy tales and fantasy novels, MacDonald inspired many authors, such as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7014283.G_K__Chesterton" title="G.K. Chesterton" rel="nofollow noopener">G.K. Chesterton</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/233593.W__H__Auden" title="W. H. Auden" rel="nofollow noopener">W. H. Auden</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/656983.J_R_R__Tolkien" title="J.R.R. Tolkien" rel="nofollow noopener">J.R.R. Tolkien</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1069006.C__S__Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener">C. S. Lewis</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/106.Madeleine_L_Engle" title="Madeleine L'Engle" rel="nofollow noopener">Madeleine L'Engle</a>. Lewis wrote that he regarded MacDonald as his "master": "Picking up a copy of <i>Phantastes</i> one day at a train-station bookstall, I began to read. A few hours later," said Lewis, "I knew that I had crossed a great frontier." G. K. Chesterton cited <i>The Princess and the Goblin</i> as a book that had "made a difference to my whole existence."<br /><br />Elizabeth Yates wrote of Sir Gibbie, "It moved me the way books did when, as a child, the great gates of literature began to open and first encounters with noble thoughts and utterances were unspeakably thrilling."<br /><br />Even <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1244.Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain" rel="nofollow noopener">Mark Twain</a>, who initially disliked MacDonald, became friends with him, and there is some evidence that Twain was influenced by MacDonald.<br /><br />For more information, please see <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_MacDonald">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_M...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4134414265339320724" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/19040699-sir-gibbie">Quotes from Sir Gibbie</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;No man can order his life, for it comes flowing over him from behind. But if it lay before us, and we could watch its current approaching from a long distance, what could we do with it before it had reached the now? In like wise a man thinks foolishly who imagines he could have done this and that with his own character and development, if he had but known this and that in time.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/953876-no-man-can-order-his-life-for-it-comes-flowing">4 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;But I withhold my pen; for vain were the fancy, by treatise or sermon or poem or tale, to persuade a man to forget himself. He cannot if he would. Sooner will he forget the presence of a raging tooth. There is no forgetting of ourselves but in the finding of our deeper, our true self -- God's idea of us when he devised us -- the Christ in us. Nothing but that self can displace the false, greedy, whining self, of which, most of us are so fond and proud. And that self no man can find for himself; seeing of himself he does not even know what to search for.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/953868-but-i-withhold-my-pen-for-vain-were-the-fancy">3 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
